  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer17236751256768471221">Henry Pulling, a retired bank manager, meets his septuagenarian Aunt Augusta for the first time in over fifty years at what he supposes to be his mother's funeral. Soon after, she persuades Henry to abandon Southwood, his dahlias and the Major next door to travel her way, Brighton, Paris, Istanbul, Paraguay. Through Aunt Augusta, a veteran of Europe's hotel bedrooms, Henry</span>
  <span id="freeText17236751256768471221" style="display:none">Henry Pulling, a retired bank manager, meets his septuagenarian Aunt Augusta for the first time in over fifty years at what he supposes to be his mother's funeral. Soon after, she persuades Henry to abandon Southwood, his dahlias and the Major next door to travel her way, Brighton, Paris, Istanbul, Paraguay. Through Aunt Augusta, a veteran of Europe's hotel bedrooms, Henry joins a shiftless, twilight society: mixing with hippies, war criminals, CIA men; smoking pot, breaking all the currency regulations and eventually coming alive after a dull suburban life. In <i>Travels with my Aunt</i> Graham Greene not only gives us intoxicating entertainment but also confronts us with some of the most perplexing of human dilemmas.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17236751256768471221" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1733901007" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1733901007" />
    <a title="Vit Babenco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco"><img alt="Vit Babenco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365604577p2/19283284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1733901007?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vit Babenco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vit Babenco" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco">Vit Babenco</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670725243" href="/book/show/771917.Travels_with_My_Aunt">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1733901007" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer675530291243344194">Some persons are like cats and some – like mice…<br/><blockquote>…and in any case I have a weakness for funerals. People are generally seen at their best on these occasions, serious and sober, and optimistic on the subject of personal immortality.</blockquote><br/><b>Graham Greene</b> has at once won my attention with his subtle irony – for me it is the best kind of wit.<br/>Protagonist and narrator, Henry Pulling, a retired bank manager is a very timorous and highly introvertive man.<br/>This is the boy:<br/><blockquote>Too many books by too many authors can be </blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText675530291243344194" style="display:none">Some persons are like cats and some – like mice…<br /><blockquote>…and in any case I have a weakness for funerals. People are generally seen at their best on these occasions, serious and sober, and optimistic on the subject of personal immortality.</blockquote><br /><b>Graham Greene</b> has at once won my attention with his subtle irony – for me it is the best kind of wit.<br />Protagonist and narrator, Henry Pulling, a retired bank manager is a very timorous and highly introvertive man.<br />This is the boy:<br /><blockquote>Too many books by too many authors can be confusing, like too many shirts and suits. I like to change my clothes as little as possible. I suppose some people would say the same of my ideas, but the bank had taught me to be wary of whims. Whims so often end in bankruptcy.</blockquote><br />His aunt, Aunt Augusta is a woman of the world, she is very extravertive and she knows no scruples.<br />This is the girl:<br /><blockquote>I remembered how at Brighton she had told me that her idea of fame was to be represented at Tussaud’s, dressed in one of her own costumes, and I really believe she would have opted for the Chamber of Horrors rather than have had no image made of her at all.</blockquote><br />So thrown together they constitute quite an alliance…<br /><blockquote>It was as though I had escaped from an open prison, had been snatched away, provided with a rope ladder and a waiting car, into my aunt’s world, the world of the unexpected character and the unforeseen event.</blockquote><br /><i>Travels With My Aunt</i> is a weird, witty mystery and for me it turned out to be a real delight.</span>
  <a data-text-id="675530291243344194" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_993091084" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/993091084" />
    <a title="Rowena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena"><img alt="Rowena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499471926p2/2990642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/993091084?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rowena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rowena" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena">Rowena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099282587" href="/book/show/824390.Travels_With_My_Aunt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2990642-rowena?shelf=english-lit">english-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2990642-rowena?shelf=bookclub-reads">bookclub-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer993091084" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3824984805649133035"> <b>"One's life is more formed, I sometimes think, by books than by human beings: it is out of books one learns about love and pain at second hand. Even if we have the happy chance to fall in love, it is because we have been conditioned by what we have read..." </b>- Graham Greene, Travels With my Aunt<br/><br/>Having only read one other Graham Greene book previously (Brighton Rock) I wasn't quite sure what to expect from this book. It turned out to be a fun and entertaining story about Henry Puling, a very unim</span>
  <span id="freeText3824984805649133035" style="display:none"> <b>"One's life is more formed, I sometimes think, by books than by human beings: it is out of books one learns about love and pain at second hand. Even if we have the happy chance to fall in love, it is because we have been conditioned by what we have read..." </b>- Graham Greene, Travels With my Aunt<br /><br />Having only read one other Graham Greene book previously (Brighton Rock) I wasn't quite sure what to expect from this book. It turned out to be a fun and entertaining story about Henry Puling, a very unimaginative, conservative retired English bachelor in his 50s who meets his eccentric Aunt Augusta for the first time in decades on the day of his mother's funeral. Aunt Augusta is one of the most unforgettable characters I've ever come across in fiction; she's selfish, unapologetic, and has had quite the unconventional life, especially if you consider that she's in her mid-70s and this story takes place in the late 1960s. She takes Henry away from his boring humdrum life of tending dahlias, and they end up travelling around the world, breaking laws and meeting a motley crowd.<br /><br />There was a lot of dry humour in this book which seems to have stood the test of time. While in Turkey Aunt Augusta says, "Politics in Turkey are taken more seriously than they are at home. It was only quite recently that they executed a Prime Minister. We dream of it, but they act." Well, it made me laugh!<br /><br />The mildly infuriating Aunt Augusta is definitely a people person and loves to tell stories. How true they are, Henry still isn't quite sure. Yet, as he later muses:<br /><br /> <b>"What does the truth matter? All characters once dead, if they continue to exist in memory at all, tend to become fictions. Hamlet is no less real now than Winston Churchill, and Joe Pulling no less historical than Don Quixote."</b><br /><br />In between all the shenanigans, Greene leaves some food for thought:<br /><br /> <b>"Human communication, it sometimes seems to me, involves an exaggerated amount of time. How briefly and to the point people always seem to speak on the stage or on the screen, while in real life we stumble from phrase to phrase with endless repetition." </b><br /><br />There's still some things I haven't figured out about this book yet. I feel Greene packed a lot more social commentary in here than my bookclub and I had time to discuss. Firstly, I felt he was poking fun at the postcolonial, post-War era, but I don't know enough about England at this time to confirm this. But maybe I wasn't meant to take the novel as seriously as I did at times.<br /><br />One part did shock me though. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Why on earth did Greene have Henry engaged to a 14 year old South American girl (they will marry as soon as she turns 16) right on the last page? What is this? I found that deeply disturbing and want to know why Greene added that part, especially right at the end  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="3824984805649133035" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer660649099" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14150081940782172853">Clever and witty, a character driven novel written in a crisp clean style. Fun comes from the interplay between stodgy Henry and his outrageous Aunt. Told through Henry’s eyes, a cautious man recently retired from banking who never married, whose passion has never extended beyond the growing of dahlias.<i> “I like to change my clothes as little as possible. I suppose some people would say the same of my ideas, the bank had taught me to be wary of whims.” </i><br/>Contrasted with Aunt Augusta who first appea</span>
  <span id="freeText14150081940782172853" style="display:none">Clever and witty, a character driven novel written in a crisp clean style. Fun comes from the interplay between stodgy Henry and his outrageous Aunt. Told through Henry’s eyes, a cautious man recently retired from banking who never married, whose passion has never extended beyond the growing of dahlias.<i> “I like to change my clothes as little as possible. I suppose some people would say the same of my ideas, the bank had taught me to be wary of whims.” </i><br />Contrasted with Aunt Augusta who first appears at his mothers funeral, an immoral woman with one driving ambition <b>- live life to the fullest.</b> Making no apologies for her self absorption she leaves in her wake a trail of broken hearts. Brutally honest <i> “I've never wanted a man who needed me, Henry. A need is a claim” </i>she simply is who she is, takes full responsibility for her actions and casts no blame. <br /><b>
  <i>'I despise no one, no one. Regret your own actions, if you like that kind of wallowing in self-pity, but never, never despise.” </i>
</b> <br />Henry's life is irreversibly changed when he joins her as a travelling companion, entering<i> “my aunt's world, the world of the unexpected character and the unforeseen event.’ </i><br /><br /><b>As British Humour a solid 4 stars.</b> My first but not last Graham Greene, think <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/133394.Our_Man_in_Havana" title="Our Man in Havana by Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">Our Man in Havana</a> next. <br /><br /><b>Cons: </b> A tad dated (but not annoyingly so) and the plot is a bit weak. If you’re the kind who writes off old people as boring you’ll really hate it, but I'm telling you - you'll be missing out on some deliciously funny stories. And finally parts of it are sorta sick <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ ends with 65 yr old Henry engaged to a 16 year old virgin, yuk  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> It’s obvious Graham was just having fun writing this - don’t take it too seriously - he clearly didn’t. <br />________________________________________<br /><i>“Laziness and good nature often go together.”</i></span>

<div id="review_2375272784" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2375272784" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41609617-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539156508p2/41609617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2375272784?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/41609617-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099282587" href="/book/show/824390.Travels_With_My_Aunt">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2375272784" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1751453073699164826">‘Travels with my Aunt’ (1969) is certainly the funniest book by Graham Greene that I have read so far. It tells us the entertaining story of Henry Pulling our very conservative, socially compliant, dull and boring erstwhile bank manager of some years standing. Henry encounters the eponymous ‘Aunt’ – Augusta for the first time in 50 years and as the title suggests, almost involuntarily, embarks on said ‘travels’.<br/><br/>So whilst at first glance ‘Travels with my Aunt’ is ostensibly not as profound nor in</span>
  <span id="freeText1751453073699164826" style="display:none">‘Travels with my Aunt’ (1969) is certainly the funniest book by Graham Greene that I have read so far. It tells us the entertaining story of Henry Pulling our very conservative, socially compliant, dull and boring erstwhile bank manager of some years standing. Henry encounters the eponymous ‘Aunt’ – Augusta for the first time in 50 years and as the title suggests, almost involuntarily, embarks on said ‘travels’.<br /><br />So whilst at first glance ‘Travels with my Aunt’ is ostensibly not as profound nor in the same league as Greene’s classics (Power and the Glory, Heart of the Matter, End of the Affair etc) – it is a very much a different kind of novel. But don’t be fooled by this veneer of a seemingly light-hearted and superficial fun story – there meaning here too.<br /><br />Amusing and entertaining though this novel is (being one of Greene’s so-called ‘entertainments’ rather than serious novels) – as it comes from the pen of Graham Greene, there is of course a serious nature and undertone to the story. There is much here about the dullness and self-imposed imprisonment of suburban domestic life – focussing on this aspect of an imprisoning effect, being happy yet bored, successful yet uninspired, an absence or suppression of any sense of adventure. What is painted here is very much a middle England, middle class, middle brow, middle management existence – certainly as the starting point and impetuous for our forthcoming adventure.<br /><br />As with all of Greene’s work, ‘Travels with my Aunt’ is expertly executed from start to finish – Greene is very much a solid and reliable, as well as brilliant, writer. Both Aunt Augusta and Henry Pulling are so very well created and drawn and when it all comes down to it – don’t we all secretly wish for our very own Aunt Augusta and a series of perplexing but exciting and life changing adventures to call our own? It that sense at least, it is not just Henry who is escaping here – it is the reader also who, as with the best of novels, is on a real journey of escapism and discovery here.<br /><br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1751453073699164826" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3069872231" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3069872231" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3069872231?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670725243" href="/book/show/771917.Travels_with_My_Aunt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=great-britain">great-britain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3069872231" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14717564049554373001">Travels with My Aunt, one of Graham Greene's later and lighter novels, was my fourth by the author, and although it might not have been the best it was certainly the most fun to read. It’s a merry one for Greene, who is normally associated with darker moralistic themes usually playing heavy on Catholicism. At the heart of the story is bank manager Henry Pulling, retired, in his 50s, whose boring suburban life, including having an affection for dahlias, is changed by his elderly Aunt Augusta, who</span>
  <span id="freeText14717564049554373001" style="display:none">Travels with My Aunt, one of Graham Greene's later and lighter novels, was my fourth by the author, and although it might not have been the best it was certainly the most fun to read. It’s a merry one for Greene, who is normally associated with darker moralistic themes usually playing heavy on Catholicism. At the heart of the story is bank manager Henry Pulling, retired, in his 50s, whose boring suburban life, including having an affection for dahlias, is changed by his elderly Aunt Augusta, who convinces him to go travelling with her. What we have then is a uproarious romp of sorts, starting in England, moving across Europe, and then later ending in Paraguay, and as the narrative progresses the comical and lighter moments (and there are plenty of them) give way to a slightly more menacing mood. What is it to lead a good life? That seems to be Greene's pondering question here. Is enthusiasm for adventure a better way of living than a quiet, comfortable life? How far would one go to upgrade from a mediocre existence ? At the funeral of his mother we first meet Henry's Aunt Augusta, and after decades of not really knowing her, she takes him back to her apartment, where she resides with her lover, a middle-aged man from Sierra Leone called Wordsworth. Aunt Augusta, who is one way or another is dabbling in some dodgy money-making scam, tells outrageous tales of her life, and it seems there’s always some truth to them, but the naive Henry, is too lacking in worldly experience to fully understand the implications of some of her actions. Suddenly Henry eyes are open to a different more exciting way of living, with his loveless existence being juxtaposed against his aunt’s often fascinating past. The travels through France, switzerland and Italy, on the way to İstanbul via the Orient Express, see others enter the story, like Tooley an American girl on the loose, her CIA father, a fortune-teller, and a war criminal, and when the scenery is dull Augusta passes the time by recalling her earlier lovers. Added to the travelling we have a police investigation into Henry’s mother’s ashes being replaced with marijuana, a spoonful of espionage, and some truly funny one-liners. I get the sense Greene got much enjoyment from creating his two central characters, who play off each other as polar opposite in various amusing set pieces along the way. I didn't find the last third as good as the previous two, but Travels with My Aunt was still a novel I much enjoyed. I would be surprised if Greene wrote anything else as humorous as this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14717564049554373001" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_73292562" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/73292562" />
    <a title="Smiley " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2580971-smiley"><img alt="Smiley " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1525349534p2/2580971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/73292562?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Smiley " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Smiley " href="/user/show/2580971-smiley">Smiley </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099282587" href="/book/show/824390.Travels_With_My_Aunt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2580971-smiley?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer73292562" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5433644722442194264">Since some years ago I’ve tried to read this seemingly readable “Travels With My Aunt” but it’s a pity I could read no more than 8-10 pages and left it on its stack, more than once. So last month I decided to read it hoping to enjoy this fiction like his six ones, I’ve found his ‘intoxicating entertainment’ (GR synopsis) amazing and worth spending my time. Like I said somewhere, I started by reading its brief synopsis as an essential overview as well as the one from Wikipedia at <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Travels_with_My_Aunt'>https://en.wikip</a></span>
  <span id="freeText5433644722442194264" style="display:none">Since some years ago I’ve tried to read this seemingly readable “Travels With My Aunt” but it’s a pity I could read no more than 8-10 pages and left it on its stack, more than once. So last month I decided to read it hoping to enjoy this fiction like his six ones, I’ve found his ‘intoxicating entertainment’ (GR synopsis) amazing and worth spending my time. Like I said somewhere, I started by reading its brief synopsis as an essential overview as well as the one from Wikipedia at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Travels_with_My_Aunt">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Travels...</a> for more detail.<br /><br />The story is about a middle-aged retired bank manager named Henry Pulling, he has just met Aunt Augusta for the first time in fifty years at his mother's funeral. His aunt, in her seventies, is a formidable character fond of Henry and action, she simply plans her itinerary abroad with a bit of adventure in mind, rather than mope and stay home; therefore, Henry has no choice but follows her plan by keeping going and solving problems from some unexpected plights or weird people along the way.<br /><br />Surprisingly, I found reading this reluctantly long-awaited book inimitably hilarious with wonderful dialogs, fantastic plot, unthinkable climax, etc. Moreover, each reasonably manageable length of each chapter is not too tedious for us as his admirers or newcomers. There are 20 chapters in Part One and only 8 in Part Two; we may call such a chapter as a numerical one because we see only Numbers 1 (4+pages), 2 (3+), 3 (8), …. 20 (6+) and Numbers 1 (10+pages), 2 (4), 3 (11), … 8 (8).<br /><br />Once in a while, I have sometime found some words used in the right context and wondered if this is one of the ways in which Greene has told us that he regards writing some of his novels as a sort of 'entertainment' that implies reading entertainment for us as well, for example:<br /><br />1. It was a sad occasion without Sir Alfred, who had been a very jovial man, laughing immoderately even at his own jokes. (p. 22)<br />2. 'How was the mowing-machine by the way?'<br /> 'Very wet, but no irreparable damage.' (p. 24)<br />3. 'I told Jo what the doctor said, and he mouthed a reply, I thought I made out, ''not good enough.'' (p. 56)<br />4. 'Does he speak English or French?'<br /> 'It is not likely.' I felt hopelessly abroad. (p. 91)<br />5. So I sat in the West Berlin Hotel shedding beery tears of self-pity and envying the men who danced with their arms round strangers' shoulders. (p. 124)<br /><br />If you notice something uniquely well-expressed in each item, you'd see the point and agree with me on the following: immoderately, irreparable, mouthed, hopelessly abroad, and beery. What do you think?<br /><br />Moreover, Greene has Wordsworth, a key character, speak his transcribed pidgin English which is of course literally amusing whenever we hear the typical dialog or we speak it mockingly. Try reading the extracts and you'd see why:<br /><br />'Ýour auntie, Mr Pullen. She allays safe with old Wordsworth. Ar no cost her nothing. But she got a fellah now -- he cost her plenty plenty. And he too old for her, Mr Pullen. Your auntie no chicken. She need a young fellah.'<br />'You aren't exactly young yourself, Wordsworth.'<br />'Ar no got ma big feet in no tomb, Mr Pullen, lak that one. Ar no trust that fellah. ...' (p. 208)<br /><br />'Who is this man she's with, Wordsworth?'<br />'I won spik his name. My tongue turn up if I spik his name. Oh, man, I bin faithful to your auntie long time now.'<br />... (p. 208)<br /><br />'He was asking me about you. He saw us on shore.'<br />'What he look lak?'<br />... (p. 209)<br /><br />Incidentally, touched by his mention of 'Thailand' in this book rather than 'Siam' as found in his memoir, I think first it's a kind of honor to see him write/type our country to the world to see and probably those people unfamiliar with or rarely heard of our country may find out in a reference or on Wikipedia, and second it's due to its first publication in 1969 so 'Thailand' has since been widely heard and more collaborated in telecommunications, journalism, business, etc. internationally. The mention in question is as follows:<br /><br />'You've been out here for six years?'<br />'No, but I was in Thailand before this.'<br />'Doing research?'<br />'Yeah. Sort of ...' ... (p. 204)<br /><br />Again, when I casually read this sentence, "The great gates had been cleaned of rust and flung open; the chandeliers sparkled in the sala, lights were turned on in even the empty room, ..." (p. 254) The word 'sala' (in italics) rang a bell and kept me wondering if it comes from a foreign or a Thai word ; so I tried Wiktionary and found two meanings: <br /><br />1. From Spanish, from Germanic; ...<br /> A large hall or reception room.<br />2. Borrowing from Thai ศาลา (saa-la).<br /> An open pavilion in Thailand used as a meeting place or to shelter from the weather.<br />[<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sala">https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sala</a>] <br /><br />I have no further information on his 'sala' used in the sentence so it might have been from either one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5433644722442194264" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_208073285" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/208073285" />
    <a title="Mariel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/647547-mariel"><img alt="Mariel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451321467p2/647547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/208073285?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mariel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mariel" href="/user/show/647547-mariel">Mariel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">The Golden Girls</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mariel by: </span>
      eenie meenie miney mo
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer208073285" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11604258913751690636">Travels with My Aunt was my first Graham Greene (films don't count! Or do they?) . I didn't know which to choose because I didn't have internet access at the time of the big moment. The jacket said it was the only book that Greene ever wrote for the fun of it. <br/><br/>Maybe he had fun. I sure as heck didn't. Maybe it was the times (publication date is 1969) ... An old woman who proclaims way too loudly that she's having a great time to make her cliche of a stiff upper lip Englishman nephew feel more bef</span>
  <span id="freeText11604258913751690636" style="display:none">Travels with My Aunt was my first Graham Greene (films don't count! Or do they?) . I didn't know which to choose because I didn't have internet access at the time of the big moment. The jacket said it was the only book that Greene ever wrote for the fun of it. <br /><br />Maybe he had fun. I sure as heck didn't. Maybe it was the times (publication date is 1969) ... An old woman who proclaims way too loudly that she's having a great time to make her cliche of a stiff upper lip Englishman nephew feel more befuddled than Hugh Grant at the height of his befuddled niche as the go-to guy for befuddled Englishmen in postcard English life films. Maybe I'm in a bad mood and this was funny in 1969. I thought that it was trying too hard to have fun. Henry didn't know how to have fun and Aunt Augusta is the aging bar slut who brags about what a crazy wild night she had fifteen years ago. I can't stand that type. Have fun while you are having it. No, I don't want to see photos of you getting drunk last week on your myspace or facebook. I was sooooo bored. I didn't care about anything that happened. Their travels were more boring than the most boring part of travels (the traveling part and not the getting somewhere part). There's a tacked on murder that came too late to be interesting. By that time I was desperate for the book to be over. Then he gets together with a flipping fifteen year old and they read religious passages from Browning. Why go through all of that just to creep me out? If he was dissatisfied with his life why not learn about women by hanging out with one who is not in her seventies and related to him? How come Aunt Augusta liked to talk about having fun so much? Talk, talk, talk. <br /><br />Could Greene have been having that much fun if he wanted to fit in so badly? All of those drugs and swinging parties? The Coleridge joke about the manservant Wordsworth was also painfully obvious.<br /><br /> Okay, now that I've read Pnin by Nabokov that has a complimentary quote by Greene on the book jacket I feel guilty trashing this book. It's kinda sad to try desperately to have fun and not be in on the joke. That doesn't mean I don't find the memory of this book boring as waiting (I hate waiting). At least it makes the whole process seem like an exercise in fun and less than preachy Aunt Augusta and her high wheeling life. Like documentaries about free love, you know?</span>
  <a data-text-id="11604258913751690636" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer9553418633956180909">A fun, rollicking story of a fun aunt. <br/><br/>The plot concerns middle-aged retired banker’s travels in Europe and South America with his quirky Aunt whom he meets after his mother’s funeral. Henry has a quiet, English sort of life. His aunt, though, is a completely different. She tells him later his mother was not his mother<br/><br/>We can guess who the birth mother is can’t we? From this point on, she engages Henry in her various travels which become increasingly illegal and dangerous. <br/><br/>It is a first person st</span>
  <span id="freeText9553418633956180909" style="display:none">A fun, rollicking story of a fun aunt. <br /><br />The plot concerns middle-aged retired banker’s travels in Europe and South America with his quirky Aunt whom he meets after his mother’s funeral. Henry has a quiet, English sort of life. His aunt, though, is a completely different. She tells him later his mother was not his mother<br /><br />We can guess who the birth mother is can’t we? From this point on, she engages Henry in her various travels which become increasingly illegal and dangerous. <br /><br />It is a first person story narrated by Henry. He is naive. <br />We suspect things that Henry, in his inexperienced and conservative ways doesn't understand. Part of the story’s enjoyment is the tension Greene creates between Henry and his free-wheeling Aunt. This tension provides one of the most fun books. <br /><br />I recommend</span>

<div id="review_2378749832" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2378749832" />
    <a title="Sharon Barrow Wilfong" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16212447-sharon-barrow-wilfong"><img alt="Sharon Barrow Wilfong" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599108473p2/16212447.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2378749832?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sharon Barrow Wilfong" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sharon Barrow Wilfong" href="/user/show/16212447-sharon-barrow-wilfong">Sharon Barrow Wilfong</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2378749832" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6730570133917557190">I thought this book would be a non-fiction travelogue of driving around Europe with Graham Greene and an aunt of his.<br/><br/>Wrong. It is a fictitious account of Henry Pulling a never-married bachelor in his fifties whose greatest adventure has been creating accounts for wealthy clients at his bank. He has now retired and enjoys quiet days cultivating his precious dahlias. Then his aunt arrives on the scene.<br/><br/>It starts at the funeral of his mother. While Pulling is sitting there in the crematorium funeral</span>
  <span id="freeText6730570133917557190" style="display:none">I thought this book would be a non-fiction travelogue of driving around Europe with Graham Greene and an aunt of his.<br /><br />Wrong. It is a fictitious account of Henry Pulling a never-married bachelor in his fifties whose greatest adventure has been creating accounts for wealthy clients at his bank. He has now retired and enjoys quiet days cultivating his precious dahlias. Then his aunt arrives on the scene.<br /><br />It starts at the funeral of his mother. While Pulling is sitting there in the crematorium funeral parlor considering his mother's life and also his father's he hears a voice behind him say, "I once attended a premature cremation."<br /><br />Thus is his introduction to his Aunt Augusta, his mother's sister.<br /><br />With no more introduction than that, Henry finds himself sucked into the drama of this aunt he has never before met. The second thing she informs him of (the first being the crematorium incident; as a child she accidentally pushed a button which set the coffin off, but luckily when the others arrived for the service, no one realized the body was no longer there) is that his mother was not his biological mother she was just the person who married his father (who was his biological father) and raised him as his own.<br /><br />The story then proceeds to bounce back between hilarity and absurdity.<br /><br />Aunt Augusta is quite a woman. Or a trollope, depending on your point of view. She has known quite a few men in her seventy odd years and is not slowing down any time soon. She is currently living with a man, Wordsworth, from an African country-quite shocking since this was published in 1970- and he's half her age. <br /><br />But before Wordsworth, she lived in Paris with a married man, and before that in Istanbul with a general Abdul and before that with an Italian Visconti and I almost forgot Currin, the priest of the Dog church back in a small English town.<br /><br />Pulling just wants to stay home with his dahlias but Aunt Augusta propels him across Europe because, it turns out, she is smuggling money and needs his help. He helps but not intentionally. Only later does he find out what she's carrying in all those heavy suitcases.<br /><br />Greene is a brilliant writer and very, very witty. But he also demonstrates how evil looks interesting in fiction when it is actually boring in real life.<br /><br />We find out that Aunt Augusta has all these lovers because she financially supplies them with her wealth. She's not stupid. She knows that is why they love her and when her money runs out, they leave her. She loves them all the more for that. She tells her nephew that she could not love a man who loved her back. Emotional need is too much of a claim on one's soul.<br /><br />It reminds me of the socially awkward kid at school who tries to buy friends with his lunch money. How is one exactly satisfied with that? The whole thing seems a sham.<br /><br />But that is not how Greene presents it. Aunt Augusta is the exciting one. Pulling is the boring one because he wants a normal secure life.<br /><br />Aunt Augusta breaks a lot of laws for the sake of her lovers and she finally ends up in Paraguay back with Visconti whom we're supposed to believe is the real love of her life. Well, as long as that smuggling business stays profitable.<br /><br />Another thing. The woman who raised Pulling is presented as a narrow-minded prig of a person and it turns out that SPOILER ALERT!!!!!! Aunt Augusta is not really is aunt, but rather his mother. This is never explicitly stated but we're to gather that from the clues strewn throughout the story.<br /><br />Excuse me, but I have to applaud the woman who raised Henry, not the woman who deserted him to traipse across the world buying criminally-minded men's love.<br /><br />But I suppose we're not really supposed to take any of it seriously. In which case it is nothing more than a well-written silly story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6730570133917557190" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1888748045" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1888748045" />
    <a title="Daniela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/62181206-daniela"><img alt="Daniela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1527432849p2/62181206.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1888748045?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniela" href="/user/show/62181206-daniela">Daniela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1888748045" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5078490191005230741">3.5* <br/><br/> <i>Travels with My Aunt</i> really cemented my love for Graham Greene. I had previously read two of his books, and I always had the nagging suspicion that he was a very witty writer, but the drama and the somber aspect of the novels I read didn’t give him much space to indulge in witticisms. <i>Travels with my Aunt</i> has all the ingredients of a very funny tale: an eccentric, sexually liberated woman, an uptight conservative nephew, colorful characters, love, intrigue, and shifty Italians. It also fee</span>
  <span id="freeText5078490191005230741" style="display:none">3.5* <br /><br /> <i>Travels with My Aunt</i> really cemented my love for Graham Greene. I had previously read two of his books, and I always had the nagging suspicion that he was a very witty writer, but the drama and the somber aspect of the novels I read didn’t give him much space to indulge in witticisms. <i>Travels with my Aunt</i> has all the ingredients of a very funny tale: an eccentric, sexually liberated woman, an uptight conservative nephew, colorful characters, love, intrigue, and shifty Italians. It also feels very modern. The other books I’ve read by Greene were set during or before WWII. This is one is set in the late 60s, much closer to our own time. <br /><br />Sadly, the last 50 pages were a letdown. Aunt Augusta was definitely the best thing about the novel and to hide her from the readers for so long did the book no favours. I enjoyed the ending, however. Although Henry’s change towards learning how to live the good life was predictable, the novel travels through his transformation in a very competent way.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1289527189" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1289527189" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1289527189?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=england">england</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=travel">travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=returned">returned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2015-read">2015-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=turkey">turkey</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=paraguay">paraguay</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1289527189" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3958545088340066768">Definitely funny.....but maybe too funny? Do you know what I mean? <br/><br/>Of course I chuckled at lines like these:<br/><br/><br/>"You will never persuade a mouse that a black cat is lucky." (chapter 5)<br/><br/>or<br/><br/>"I had such a good memory.......once!" (chapter 6)<br/><br/>or <br/><br/>"I have never planned anything illegal in my life! How could I plan anything of the kind, when I have never read any of the laws and have no idea what they are?!" (chapter 7)<br/><br/>or<br/><br/>"A little honest thieving hurts no one." And then, "It was all very harmless and gave emp</span>
  <span id="freeText3958545088340066768" style="display:none">Definitely funny.....but maybe too funny? Do you know what I mean? <br /><br />Of course I chuckled at lines like these:<br /><br /><br />"You will never persuade a mouse that a black cat is lucky." (chapter 5)<br /><br />or<br /><br />"I had such a good memory.......once!" (chapter 6)<br /><br />or <br /><br />"I have never planned anything illegal in my life! How could I plan anything of the kind, when I have never read any of the laws and have no idea what they are?!" (chapter 7)<br /><br />or<br /><br />"A little honest thieving hurts no one." And then, "It was all very harmless and gave employment to many."(chap 8)<br /><br /><br />Have you noted how the statements get more and more criminal in tone? Can Graham Greene write a book without turning it into a mystery or a crime novel? <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Interpol, smuggling, art theft and counterfeit are on display here! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. What exactly is the relationship between Aunt Augusta and her nephew, Henry? It helps to enjoy crime mystery novels. Here you get an amusing spoof. <br /><br />Back to the humor. I read somewhere that Graham Greene wrote this, his sole purpose being to compose a f-u-n-n-y book. The humor changes as the book proceeds. It becomes sharper, more satirical. Politics, sex, religion and human behavior are often the brunt of the joke. <br /><br />I would like to give you a feel for the humor because what appeals to one will be dishwater to another.... and yet I fear that you have to know the characters to understand the message conveyed. On sex, Aunt Augusta declares, keep in mind she is in her seventies, "I have always preferred an occasional orgie to a nightly routine." Or, if you are annoyed at your kids, this line might speak to you, "They go away from you. You can't go away from them." The lines are clever and funny, and certainly I chuckled often, but it is exactly that that I cannot deal with. I cannot read a joke book from start to finish.<br /><br />Have you noted that I have shelved this book in many different countries? The book is about travel and all the countries where I have shelved it are visited.....but you neither see nor smell nor experience the different couture of the lands visited. You get a teeny bit about Paraguay. The two, aunt and nephew, travel on the Oriental Express. So much more could have been done with that! <br /><br />This is a book of humor. The narrator of the audiobook, Tim Pigott-Smith, did an absolutely marvelous job of revealing that humor. He uses different intonations for the different characters in a wonderful way. Five stars for the narration. <br /><br />Please keep in mind that you may totally love this book even if it was not a good fit for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3958545088340066768" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1769925048" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1769925048" />
    <a title="WhatIReallyRead" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52500467-whatireallyread"><img alt="WhatIReallyRead" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483674713p2/52500467.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1769925048?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WhatIReallyRead" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WhatIReallyRead" href="/user/show/52500467-whatireallyread">WhatIReallyRead</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52500467-whatireallyread?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52500467-whatireallyread?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52500467-whatireallyread?shelf=boring">boring</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52500467-whatireallyread?shelf=physical-book">physical-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52500467-whatireallyread?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52500467-whatireallyread?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52500467-whatireallyread?shelf=read-in-english">read-in-english</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1769925048" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13949416547068587207"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1495890050i/22865051._SY540_.jpg' alt='Travels with My Aunt by Graham Greene - Penguin Classics ' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><b>
  <i>'Does he speak English or French?'<br/>'It is not likely.' I felt hopelessly abroad.</i>
</b><br/><br/>The book is unequally divided into two parts, the first taking up most of it. I will have to separate them in my review, for they inspired very different feelings.<br/><br/><u>The Good Stuff (about Part 1)</u><br/><br/><b>
  <i>"You must surrender yourself first to extravagance"</i>
</b><br/><br/>- it is well written, as you expect a classic work of literature to be;<br/>- it was funny, even outrageous and surprising at times, in a way I didn't expect a classic work of</span>
  <span id="freeText13949416547068587207" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1495890050i/22865051._SY540_.jpg" alt="Travels with My Aunt by Graham Greene - Penguin Classics " class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>
  <i>'Does he speak English or French?'<br>'It is not likely.' I felt hopelessly abroad.</i>
</b><br><br>The book is unequally divided into two parts, the first taking up most of it. I will have to separate them in my review, for they inspired very different feelings.<br><br><u>The Good Stuff (about Part 1)</u><br><br><b>
  <i>"You must surrender yourself first to extravagance"</i>
</b><br><br>- it is well written, as you expect a classic work of literature to be;<br>- it was funny, even outrageous and surprising at times, in a way I didn't expect a classic work of literature to be;<br>- Aunt Augusta's character;<br>- the message.<br><br><b>
  <i>"A long life is not a question of years. A man without memories might reach the age of a hundred and feel that his life had been a very brief one."<br><br>"I was afraid of burglars and Indian thugs and snakes and fires and Jack the Ripper, when I should have been afraid of thirty years in a bank and a take-over bid and a premature retirement."</i>
</b><br><br>"Travels with my Aunt" is a story of empowerment. It urges you to shed boredom and just do stuff, go places. It inspires to question the sense of moral superiority which often comes with following the rules:<br><br><b>
  <i>"Perhaps a sense of morality is the sad compensation we learn to enjoy, like a remission for good conduct"</i>
</b><br><br>I can't say I agree that is always the case, but it definitely is sometimes. We often choose to interpret our fear, laziness, inertia and perpetual boredom as moral superiority, loyalty and a will of iron, where there is none of that.<br><br><u>Aunt Augusta</u>'s character in Part 1 can be summed up by these few lines:<br><br><b>
  <i>'I hope you don't plan anything illegal.'<br>'I have never planned anything illegal in my life', Aunt Augusta said. 'How could I plan anything of the kind when I have never read any of the laws and have no idea what they are?'</i>
</b><br><br>Aunt Augusta is a 75-y.o. lady and a spitfire. She is straightforward and honest, she loves life and she loves people. She is unashamed to state, that at her age, yes, she still falls in love and enjoys a sex life. I'm so used to seeing old people portrayed as either adorable-lost-featherhead knitting in the background, or as a cranky old fuck, or as a quintessence of faceless wisdom. Aunt Augusta is flesh and blood, and that was refreshing. Her honest, judgment-free view on other people around her was nice to read. She has her faults, for sure, but I agreed with Henry here:<br><br><b>
  <i>"Loyalty to a person inevitably entails loyalty to all the imperfections of a human being, even to the chicanery and immorality from which my aunt was not entirely free."</i>
</b><br><br><u>The Bad Stuff (about Part 1)</u><br>- it was slow at times, and I found myself zoning out;<br>- Henry's character.<br><br><u>Henry</u> is best described here: <i>"As I went upstairs to bed I felt myself to be a ghost returning home, transparent as water."</i> He's over 55, doesn't have any friends, lovers, interests, skills or a job or anything at all in his life. The man has never been in a relationship (romantic or just close kinship), has never been excited about ANYTHING! He studied, and after graduation, his mom found him a clerking job, which he held for over 30 years, now he has retired, and that's it! All he does is gardening and reading a few of the same books back to back over and over. I can't imagine someone really having such an empty life, such a total lack of personality, ambition, will. <br><br>This story is supposed to be about his journey to becoming a real person, of his empowerment, but in fact, he just continues with the same thing:<br><br><i>"It was as though I had escaped from an open prison, had been snatched away, provided with a rope ladder and a waiting car, into my aunt's world, the world of unexpected character and the unforeseen event"</i><br><br>Again, he just goes with the drift of things, the drift being Aunt Augusta, he makes no decisions of his own, just, as always, follows along and pretends that's what he wants.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1495890050i/22865052._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><u>The Good Stuff (about Part 2)</u><br><br>None found.<br><br><u>The Bad Stuff (about Part 2) **Spoilery**</u><br><br>The first part wasn't exactly perfect, but it was good, but it all went to shit in Part 2.<br><br><u>Aunt Augusta disappointed me so badly</u>, it was as if her whole strength was eliminated. She is head over heels in love with this guy - they've been together before, in their youth. The man is described as "short and fat and bald" - I appreciate the fact that he wasn't an 80-y.o. with a six-pack. YES, being old and not beautiful does not render you unlovable. So, she loves him. But he so obviously doesn't love her back, uses her, etc. And he's done it before. Their relationship years ago ended on him robbing her blind! <br><br><i>"- So you gave him money the second time, Aunt Augusta?<br>-Of course, what did you expect? He needed it."</i><br><br><i>'You have forgotten glasses.' I watched Aunt Augusta with fascination. I have never seen her taking orders from anyone before."</i><br><br>DAFAQ?! <b>Aunt Augusta is supposed to be this devil-may-care strong woman, femme fatale even, but in Part 2 she just loses all self-respect and allows herself to become a doormat.</b> Although to be fair, if we recall the stories she told about her romantic history with other guys - she has been there before, too. Like, with that married guy she loved, then found out she was his <i>second</i> mistress, then he dumped her, and she begged him to continue fucking her once a week, being third in line. UGH.<br><br>The author seems to romanticize females being doormats: <i>"Not many men have been so loved or have been forgiven so much."</i> Um. Let's see. So they used to be together. He dumped her and robbed her of a fortune. She went on living, and then decades later she gets a letter from him, asking her to participate in illegal activity and giving him all the money she has. What does she do? Drops everything, travels half the world over to bring him everything in her teeth and virtually becomes his servant. WOW! <br><br>And that is presented to us as an example of super-love, selflessness, kindness, forgiveness. Ahem. <b>When someone robs you, then asks for more, and you give it - that's not forgiveness. The perpetrator didn't ask for forgiveness, doesn't think he did anything wrong at all, and you affirm that.</b> UGHHHHH I was sooooooooooo riled by this whole storyline I was on the verge of screaming at my book.<br><br>Aunt Augusta continues spewing evil bullshit:<br><br><i>"I've never wanted a man who needed me, Henry"<br><br>"I need a man who is untouchable. Two touchables together, what a terrible life they always make of it, two people suffering, afraid to speak, afraid to act, afraid of hurting. Life can be bearable when it's only one who suffers. It's easy to put up with your own suffering, but not someone else's. I'm not afraid of making Mr. Visconti suffer. I wouldn't know how. I have a wonderful feeling of freedom. I can say what I like and it will never get under that thick dago skin of his"</i><br><br>Okay, lady! Don't parade your emotional dependence on this guy as liberty. Oh, you're not afraid of offending him, because he doesn't care? Well, you can't make him happy either, for the same reason. Can't love a good guy, who loves you? That's your problem if you like pining after someone who doesn't give a fuck, but don't paint the rest of the world as a bunch of suffering people. <b>Caring for one another is about HAPPINESS, not misery. It doesn't make one fearful or weak. You become stronger and you share that strength with the loved one.</b><br><br>There were a few bullshitty details for which I don't understand the purpose of this book. Like the illiterate servant who perfectly forged a valuable painting and put his initials on it. Henry meeting O'Toole in Paraguay - who is a CIA agent and the father of Tooley - the girl they briefly met on a train in Europe. Like, how likely is that? I would have bought it if Tooley was sorta spying on them... But no. Supposedly, it was pure coincidence. And O'Toole believes they were friends - for no reason whatsoever.<br><br>The predictable twist that Augusta is actually Henry's mom, not aunt. Totally saw it coming.<br><br>By the end of the book, there appeared bizarre poetry quotations on virtually every page. I didn't get them, their purpose there, it was just stupid.<br><br><b>The worst was the conclusion to it all. </b>Henry becomes the henchman of the guy Augusta "loves". Again, none of that is his decision, so the supposed emancipation is a total failure. As always, Henry just goes where others take him. So he smuggles drugs for that guy. That's presented as liberation. <b>And he becomes engaged to a 14-year old girl, to marry her at the age of 16. He's close to 60 at that point!</b> And the book takes place somewhere around the 1960s, so it's not like it was normal at the time. DAFAQ?!<br><br><b>Why do books about personal liberation have to create a conflict between law and freedom? The opposite of boredom is interest. You don't need to participate in an orgy to be an interesting person.</b> You don't need to turn an aging bank clerk into a drug smuggler! Just give him a few hobbies, a friend, let him take responsibility for his own life, be an active participant in it, not a log being hurled downstream in a river.<br><br>UGHHH</span>
  <a data-text-id="13949416547068587207" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_2197413313" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2197413313" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1895570-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484874117p2/1895570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2197413313?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/1895570-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=britain">britain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2197413313" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6579932038840261691">Somehow I thought this book was going to be a lighthearted romp. Funny it was, but in a sad, meditative way as Henry Pulling comes under the influence of his Aunt Augusta Bertram. I should have known better: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2533.Graham_Greene' title='Graham Greene' rel='nofollow noopener'>Graham Greene</a> is not the romping type. That takes a particular kind of character, one which does not look at life with the calm grey eyes of the author of <b>The Heart of the Matter</b> and <b>The Burnt-Out Case</b>.<br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48858.Travels_With_My_Aunt' title='Travels With My Aunt by Graham Greene' rel='nofollow noopener'>Travels With My Aunt</a> is a delightful book -- one that could easily have gone off in severa</span>
  <span id="freeText6579932038840261691" style="display:none">Somehow I thought this book was going to be a lighthearted romp. Funny it was, but in a sad, meditative way as Henry Pulling comes under the influence of his Aunt Augusta Bertram. I should have known better: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2533.Graham_Greene" title="Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">Graham Greene</a> is not the romping type. That takes a particular kind of character, one which does not look at life with the calm grey eyes of the author of <b>The Heart of the Matter</b> and <b>The Burnt-Out Case</b>.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/48858.Travels_With_My_Aunt" title="Travels With My Aunt by Graham Greene" rel="nofollow noopener">Travels With My Aunt</a> is a delightful book -- one that could easily have gone off in several other directions. But it didn't: With his aunt, Henry has found a family to replace the one he lost; and, with her, he has found the attractive teenage daughter of a Paraguayan customs official.<br /><br />I like to remember the late Mr Pottifer's idea of immortality:<blockquote>I think the reason lay partly in his idea of immortality, but I think too it belonged to his war against the Inland Revenue. He was a great believer in delaying tactics. "Never answer all their questions," he would say. "Make them write again. And be ambiguous. You can always decide what you mean later according to circumstances. The bigger the file the bigger the work. Personnel frequently change. A newcomer has to start looking at the file from the beginning. Office space is limited. In the end it's easier for them to give in."</blockquote>The way that Greene plants the Pottifer story in the novel gives it a unique significance. Check it out when you read the book: I don't want to give the author's secret away. I have too much respect for him.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6579932038840261691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_56644526" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56644526" />
    <a title="Evan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1248986-evan"><img alt="Evan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488574589p2/1248986.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/56644526?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evan" href="/user/show/1248986-evan">Evan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670725243" href="/book/show/771917.Travels_with_My_Aunt">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=travel">travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=__in-my-collection">__in-my-collection</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=2010-reads">2010-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=graham-greene">graham-greene</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer56644526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8316836854353055180"><i>"I found myself to be a ghost returning home, transparent as water. Curran was more alive than I was. I was almost surprised to see that my image was visible in the glass."</i><br/><br/>So says Henry Pulling, a retired English bank manager who has lived life so prudently, safely, carefully and boringly that he comes to realize that he has left no consequential living memory in anyone he's ever met. His favorite thing in all the world is tending to his dahlia flower garden and reading dusty volumes of Wordswor</span>
  <span id="freeText8316836854353055180" style="display:none"><i>"I found myself to be a ghost returning home, transparent as water. Curran was more alive than I was. I was almost surprised to see that my image was visible in the glass."</i><br /><br />So says Henry Pulling, a retired English bank manager who has lived life so prudently, safely, carefully and boringly that he comes to realize that he has left no consequential living memory in anyone he's ever met. His favorite thing in all the world is tending to his dahlia flower garden and reading dusty volumes of Wordsworth and Sir Walter Scott. When his mother dies, Pulling is suddenly clued in to a lot of realities about who he really is and what his family was really up to, thanks to a long-absent ebulliant aunt, Augusta, who brings Henry up to speed on a good number of the facts of life that the naive naif nephew seems to have missed. Aunt Augusta herself has been long absent because she's been spending her life traveling Europe, living and loving and losing with brio, and becoming entangled in romances and petty criminality, some of which may be fancifully embellished in the retelling to Henry. She whisks Henry off on various travels, and during their course, dull Henry starts to regret the wasted opportunities of his life.<br /><br />Greene generally classed his books broadly into two categories: serious novels and what he coined "entertainments." This one blends elements of both, weaving an eccentric whimsical tale with dark undertones into a typical Greene story of Anglos entwined in espionage, smuggling and foreign intrigue in Third World countries. One thing that's always interesting about Greene's novels is the timelessness of his political observations; nothing much changes even if the surface issues and names do. There's even a fictional shell corporation mentioned that sounds prophetically like Enron. As usual, Greene casts a bemused observational eye on the strange bedfellows borne of corruption and amorphous morality operating within systems, politics, ideologies. As usual, Catholicism (and other religions) enter the mix and come in for genteel digs.<br /><br />Though this book might be considered lightweight and trivial Greene to some degree, it accumulates in relevance as it proceeds, building a palpable gravitas. The book is very meticulous and slow; some might rebel in the face of its sometimes Jamesian precision of descriptive detail. The book plods a bit and seems to not have a plot, but there is one; it's just not rushed and is always hinted at with tiny clues along the way. The main point of the book, though, is about how people choose to live their lives, and it's that point of contrast that Greene explores in themes and variations. In some ways, it seems almost too simplistic to make the contrast with such obviously diametrically opposed characters; Pulling does seem too boring to be real. But as it proceeds his character takes on dimension, and the contrast between he and his Aunt provide much food for thought. Aunt Augusta, like Henry, is a literary construct, no doubt, but she's a fun one, speaking the Queen's English but letting shocking (to Henry) revelations pour from her seemingly proper old tongue.<br /><br />The humor and situations at the outset of the book seem fidgety and forced, one senses Greene trying to outdo Waugh and Wodehouse and failing, but eventually the charms emerge and there are a few genuine laughs and crazy situations and characters, such as the cremation urn used for drug smuggling and the CIA man who keeps a meticulous daily diary of his urine output.<br /><br />This novel's parts are better than the whole, and I have to admit that I found this to be my least favorite Greene book thus far (*2011 addendum: I would now say <i>Loser Takes All</i> is my least favorite GG). Yet, I think it's a better book than I'm giving it credit for; it covers a lot of ground and at the end is quite poetic. There's a major revelation in the last pages that was telegraphed long before and that's how I'd already suspected that that's where the book was going. <br /><br />The novel is a bit of an oddity and will not be everyone's cup of tea and it's definitely NOT the place to start to engage the work of Graham Greene, but readers familiar with his motifs and style will find it strong on observation, occasionally delightful and a worthwhile example of his eclectism.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8316836854353055180" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/205825896" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>
